10.1016/j.jmp.2014.01.002

                         A 
                       of alternatives is traditionally done via the construction of a reflexive binary relation on  
                         A

                         A

                         ≿

The approach that assumes a weak preference  
                         ≿ 
                       to be “fully loaded” with the ordering properties (i) and (ii) has undisputable advantages. Its most relevant feature is that, under the Debreu-separability condition, a total preorder  
                         ≿ 
                       is  representable  by a continuous real-valued utility function. (A preorder  
                         ≿ 
                       on  
                         A 
                       is  Debreu-separable  if there exists a countable set  
                         Z 
                         ⊆ 
                         A 
                       such that  
                         a 
                         ≻ 
                         b 
                       implies  
                         a 
                         ≿ 
                         z 
                         ≿ 
                         b 
                       for some  
                         z 
                         ∈ 
                         Z 
                      : see   Bridges & Mehta, 1995  for an overview of the topic.) Thus a problem of maximizing a (Debreu-separable) transitive and complete weak preference can be translated into one of maximizing a continuous real-valued function.

However, this classical approach to preference modeling has several drawbacks as well. A technical problem is related to the use of real-valued utility functions for the representation of preferences. In fact, all preferences modeled by orderings which are not embeddable into  
                         R 
                       (e.g., the real plane  
                         
                            
                               R 
                            
                            
                               2 
                            
                         
                       endowed with the lexicographic order) fail to admit a utility representation (see   Beardon, Candeal, Herden, Induráin, & Mehta, 2002  for a classification of non-representable linear orderings, and   Giarlotta, 2005  for a refinement which employs lexicographic powers as representation benchmarks). A possible solution to this problem is to employ utility representations  
                         u 
                         : 
                         A 
                         → 
                         Y 
                       having a codomain  
                         Y 
                       different from  
                         R 
                      , such as lexicographic orderings (see, e.g.,   Chipman, 1971; Knoblauch, 2000; Wakker, 1988 ). The continuity/semicontinuity (with respect to the order topology on the domain and the codomain) of these extended forms of representability has been carefully analyzed (see, e.g.,   Caserta, Giarlotta, & Watson, 2008; Giarlotta & Watson, 2009; Herden & Mehta, 2004; Wakker, 1988 ).

More important, the classical approach to preference modeling lacks generality, because it is often the case that the simultaneous satisfaction of the axioms of transitivity and completeness is inadequate to faithfully model the complexity of many scenarios. As a consequence, several new approaches based on the relaxation of (i) and/or (ii) have emerged in time. A first alternative approach consists of dropping the completeness axiom (ii), in order to allow an economic agent to be occasionally indecisive ( Aumann, 1962; Bewley, 1986; Doignon, 1988; Mandler, 2005 ). Therefore, in this case a weak preference  
                         ≿ 
                       assumes the form of a (potentially incomplete)  partial preorder . A second alternative approach is to require that a weak preference  
                         ≿ 
                       satisfies condition (ii), whereas transitivity holds for the strict preference  
                         ≻ 
                       but may fail for the indifference  
                         ∼ 
                       (hence for the weak preference  
                         ≿ 
                       as well). The rationale beyond this approach is that in many decision problems, intransitivity of indifference turns out to be a natural feature of the associated preference structure ( Fishburn, 1970a,b; Krantz, 1967; Shafer, 1974; Tversky, 1969 ).

Our approach belongs to the latter category, since we deal with reflexive and complete binary relations, which satisfy suitable forms of pseudo-transitivity, without necessarily being fully transitive. In this category, the most studied binary relations are  interval orders  and  semiorders , due to their capability of modeling various types of situations in a wide range of applied fields, e.g., decision-aid, mathematical psychology, choice theory under risk, genetics, information storage, scheduling, mathematical programming, archeology. (See Chapter 2 of the monograph   Pirlot & Vincke, 1997  for an extensive list of topics in which the associated preference structures can be naturally modeled by a semiorder or an interval order.)

Historically, the notion of a semiorder came first. Despite introduced earlier in the work of Wiener and Armstrong (see the historical notes in   Fishburn & Monjardet, 1992 ), the first axiomatic treatment of semiorders is due to Luce ( Luce, 1956 ). His aim was to develop a theory of preferences able to model situations of intransitive indifference with a “threshold of discrimination or perception”: Luce’s famous example of a sweetened cup of tea is a very convincing argument to employ this type of preference structure in many scenarios. Later on, Fishburn ( Fishburn, 1970b ) extended the notion of a semiorder to the more general structure of an interval order. He built preference models in which the intransitivity of the associated indifference is due to the fact that each alternative is assigned an “interval of evaluation”. Recent research in the field of preference structures with a possibly intransitive indifference has been focusing on the representability of these two types of binary relations: see, e.g.,   Abbas (1995); Bosi, Candeal, Induráin, Olóriz, and Zudaire (2001); Bosi, Candeal, Induráin, and Zudaire (2005); Bosi, Gutiérrez García, and Induráin (2009); Fishburn (1973); Oloriz, Candeal, and Induráin (1998)  for interval orders, and   Abbas, Pirlot, and Vincke (2007); Abrísqueta, Candeal, Induráin, and Zudaire (2009); Candeal and Induráin (2010); Candeal, Induráin, and Zudaire (2002); Fishburn (1973); Gensemer (1987); Manders (1981); Scott and Suppes (1958); S̀wistak (1980)  for semiorders.

Whenever interval orders and semiorders are defined (or characterized) in terms of the properties of an irreflexive binary relation  
                         ≻ 
                      , i.e., using (a) and (b), then they are asymmetric and transitive, but in general incomplete: we refer to this approach to the topic as the  strict preference approach . On the other hand, if interval orders and semiorders are defined (or characterized) by employing a reflexive relation  
                         ≿ 
                       and requiring that it satisfies, respectively, (

                         ≿ 
                       is automatically complete, but in general not transitive: we call this approach the  weak preference approach .

Despite an approach that uses a reflexive preference as a primitive relation appears in some papers, the majority of the related literature either employs the strict preference approach or separates the analysis of strict preference from that of indifference: see, e.g., the original papers by Luce and Fishburn, the monographs on interval orders ( Fishburn, 1985 ) and on semiorders ( Pirlot & Vincke, 1997 ), as well as the recent treatise ( Aleskerov, Bouyssou, & Monjardet, 2007 ). The notion of NaP-preference was originally introduced in the realm of a multiple criteria methodology called  Robust Ordinal Regression  ( ROR ) ( Greco, Mousseau, & Słowiński, 2008; Greco, Słowiński, Mousseau, & Figueira, 2010 ). The ROR approach uses techniques of operations research to build a set  
                         U 
                       of additive value functions  
                         u 
                         : 
                         
                            
                               R 
                            
                            
                               n 
                            
                         
                         → 
                         R 
                      , which are compatible with all information provided by an economic agent on a set  
                         A 
                       of  
                         n 
                      -dimensional alternatives (i.e., in the presence of a set  
                         G 
                         = 
                         
                            { 
                            
                               
                                  g 
                               
                               
                                  i

                            i

                               1

                               …

                       of  
                         n 
                         ≥ 
                         2 
                       evaluation criteria  
                         
                            
                               g 
                            
                            
                               i

                         A 
                         → 
                         R 
                      ). In this multi-dimensional setting, the binary relations

In a recent axiomatization of this new approach to preference modeling ( Giarlotta & Greco, 2013 ), the authors prove the following result (under the Axiom of Choice): a pair  
                         
                            ( 
                            
                               
                                  ≿

NaP-preferences are closely related to the topic of this paper. In fact, classical preference relations such as interval orders and semiorders (as well as stronger versions of them) are the possible components of NaP-preferences satisfying extended forms of NaP-transitivity: see the notion of the  NaP-spectrum  of a complete preference relation in Section 5 of   Giarlotta (in press) . More generally, weak  
                         
                            ( 
                            m 
                            , 
                            n 
                            ) 
                         
                      -Ferrers properties are equivalent to suitable  
                         n 
                      th-order NaP-transitivity properties, which are obtained using finite sequences of  
                         
                            
                               ≻ 
                            
                            
                               
                                  
                                  
                                     
                                        
                                        
                                           N 
                                        
                                     
                                  
                               
                            
                         
                       and  
                         
                            
                               ≿ 
                            
                            
                               
                                  
                                  
                                     
                                        
                                        
                                           P 
                                        
                                     
                                  
                               
                            
                         
                      .

The paper is organized as follows. In Section   2  we compare strict and weak  
                         
                            ( 
                            m 
                            , 
                            n 
                            ) 
                         
                      -Ferrers properties to each other, emphasizing their dual behavior as well as the irregularities of strict  
                         
                            ( 
                            m 
                            , 
                            1 
                            ) 
                         
                      -Ferrers properties. In Section   3  we establish the finite poset of weak  
                         
                            ( 
                            m 
                            , 
                            n 
                            ) 
                         
                      -Ferrers properties, which is described in its combinatorial complexity in the  Appendix . Section   4  contains some conclusive remarks and future directions of research.

We recall some basic terminology. Henceforth the symbol  
                         ≿ 
                       denotes a reflexive binary relation on a nonempty set  
                         A 
                       of alternatives, and is generically called a  weak preference  on  
                         A 
                      . We emphasize from the outset that our analysis does not depend on the size of the ground set  
                         A 
                      . The symbols  
                         a 
                         ≿ 
                         b 
                       and  
                         b 
                         ≾ 
                         a 
                       are interchangeably used to denote  
                         
                            ( 
                            a 
                            , 
                            b 
                            ) 
                         
                         ∈ 
                         
                         ≿ 
                      . For the sake of synthesis, we slightly abuse notation and write sequences of binary relationships in a compact way: for example,  
                         a 
                         ≿ 
                         b 
                         ≿ 
                         c 
                         ≿ 
                         d 
                       means  
                         
                            { 
                            
                               ( 
                               a 
                               , 
                               b 
                               ) 
                            
                            , 
                            
                               ( 
                               b 
                               , 
                               c 
                               ) 
                            
                            , 
                            
                               ( 
                               c

                               d

                            }

                         ≿ 
                      . The notation  
                         a 
                         ∼ 
                         b

                         a 
                         ≿ 
                         b 
                       and  
                         b 
                         ≿ 
                         a 
                      , whereas  
                         a 
                         ≻

                       stands for  
                         a 
                         ≿ 
                         b 
                       and  
                         ¬ 
                         
                            ( 
                            b 
                            ≿ 
                            a 
                            ) 
                         
                      . As customary in utility theory, the two binary relations  
                         ∼ 
                       and  
                         ≻ 
                       derived from the weak preference  
                         ≿ 
                       are, respectively, the  indifference  and the  strict preference  associated with  
                         ≿ 
                      . Note that  
                         ∼ 
                       is reflexive and symmetric (but not transitive in general), whereas  
                         ≻ 
                       is irreflexive and asymmetric (and, in the majority of the cases considered in this paper, transitive as well). A  total preorder  is a reflexive, transitive and complete binary relation. A  linear order  (or  total order ) is an antisymmetric total preorder. Note that  
                         ∼ 
                       is an equivalence relation in case that  
                         ≿ 
                       is a total preorder; in particular,  
                         ∼ 
                       coincides with equality if  
                         ≿ 
                       is a linear order.

The notion of Ferrers properties given in  Definition 2.1  is very similar to that originally introduced by Öztürk in   Öztürk (2008) , yet it differs from it on a few aspects. First of all, Öztürk exclusively applies her definition to an irreflexive and asymmetric relation  
                         ≻ 
                      , whereas we employ a reflexive relation  
                         ≿ 
                       as a primitive preference and thus define two versions of Ferrers properties. Second, Öztürk postulates the finiteness of the ground set  
                         A 
                      , whereas we put no restriction on the size of  
                         A 
                       (but this difference can be overcome). A third difference is merely technical, since we use sequences of  
                         m 
                       and  
                         n 
                       elements to define what an  
                         
                            ( 
                            m 
                            , 
                            n 
                            ) 
                         
                      -Ferrers property is, instead of using sequences of, respectively,  
                         m 
                         + 
                         1 
                       and  
                         n 
                         + 
                         1 
                       elements as in   Öztürk (2008) .

Rather surprisingly, weak and strict Ferrers properties do not coincide, apart from very few exceptions. In fact, we shall show that a weak  
                         
                            ( 
                            m 
                            , 
                            n 
                            ) 
                         
                      -Ferrers property is equivalent to its strict counterpart only when  
                         m 
                         + 
                         n 
                         = 
                         4 
                      , i.e., for  
                         
                            ( 
                            m 
                            , 
                            n 
                            ) 
                         
                         ∈ 
                         
                            { 
                            
                               ( 
                               2 
                               , 
                               2 
                               ) 
                            
                            , 
                            
                               ( 
                               3 
                               , 
                               1 
                               ) 
                            
                            } 
                         
                      ; on the other hand, the two notions display a remarkably different behavior whenever  
                         m 
                         + 
                         n 
                         ≠ 
                         4 
                      . To determine the relationships between the two versions of Ferrers properties, we introduce some further definitions. We warn the reader that the terminology used here is slightly non-standard; however, we believe that it might be useful in creating a homogeneous framework for preference structures.

The next result uses the celebrated extension theorem of Szpilrajn ( Szpilrajn, 1930 ) to establish a link among the notions introduced in  Definition 2.2  (see also   Abbas & Vincke, 1993; Agaev & Aleskerov, 1993  for a characterization of acyclic simple preferences).

Proof 
                         To prove (i), assume that  
                               ≿ 
                             contains a linear order  
                               ⊵ 
                             on  
                               A

                               ≻

                               ≻

                            . Since a linear order contains no cycles, it follows that  
                               ≿

                               ≿ 
                             is an acyclic simple preference. Then the transitive closure

                               ≻

                               ≿

                               A

                               ≿

                               ≿ 
                             is an extended preference.

The relationship among the types of weak preferences  
                         ≿ 
                       mentioned so far (the classical ones and those introduced in  Definition 2.2 ) can be summarized by the chain of implications given in  Fig. 1 
                      , where  
                         ≿ 
                       is assumed to be both reflexive and complete. In the same figure we also anticipate the effect of the two types of Ferrers properties, as established later on in this paper. In fact, we shall prove that combinations of weak  
                         
                            ( 
                            m 
                            , 
                            n 
                            ) 
                         
                      -Ferrers properties entail a refinement in the upper segment of this hierarchy of weak preferences, namely, in passing from total quasi-preorders to total preorders. Conversely, combinations of strict  
                         
                            ( 
                            m 
                            , 
                            n 
                            ) 
                         
                      -properties yield a refinement in a lower segment of this hierarchy, namely, in passing from simple preferences to semiorders.

Next, we examine the reciprocal behavior of the two types of Ferrers properties, as well as their similarities and differences. To start, note that a reflexive relation is weakly  
                         
                            ( 
                            1 
                            , 
                            1 
                            ) 
                         
                      -Ferrers if and only if it is complete (i.e., a simple preference), whereas no reflexive relation satisfies the strict  
                         
                            ( 
                            1 
                            , 
                            1 
                            ) 
                         
                      -Ferrers property. The second step is to examine the two versions of the  
                         
                            ( 
                            2 
                            , 
                            1 
                            ) 
                         
                      -Ferrers property, which is done in the next result (see also  Fig. 1 ).

                             by repeating, respectively,

                             times the last element. Now the hypothesis yields

Lemmas 2.4–2.6  seem to point out a sort of dual monotonic behavior of weak and strict Ferrers properties. However, contrary to the fully monotonic (increasing) behavior of weak Ferrers properties, the monotonically decreasing strength of their strict version only holds under the condition that  
                         ≻ 
                       is transitive (see  Lemma 2.6 
                      
                         
                            
                               
                                  (i) 
                               
                            
                            
                               
                                  s 
                               
                            
                         
                      ). Indeed, in the absence of the transitivity of  
                         ≻ 
                      , the behavior of strict  
                         
                            ( 
                            m 
                            , 
                            n 
                            ) 
                         
                      -Ferrers properties turns out to be rather irregular as  
                         m 
                       and  
                         n 
                       increase. This fact is already apparent in the simplest case, i.e., for  
                         n 
                         = 
                         1 
                      . We end this section by pointing out a few irregularities of strict  
                         
                            ( 
                            m 
                            , 
                            1 
                            ) 
                         
                      -Ferrers properties.

First of all, note that the strict  
                         
                            ( 
                            m 
                            , 
                            1 
                            ) 
                         
                      -Ferrers property does not get stronger as  
                         m 
                       increases, even under the additional assumption that  
                         ≿ 
                       is a total quasi-preorder.

In this section we establish the hierarchy of weak Ferrers properties (henceforth often called “Ferrers properties”, whenever clear from context). The next result summarizes the relationships between key types of Ferrers properties. As a consequence, we obtain that the number of weak

                            , 
                            n

                      -Ferrers axioms that we may require a reflexive relation to satisfy is always finite.

A reflexive transitive relation  
                         ≿ 
                       is not complete in general, hence the converse of  Theorem 3.1 (iv) does not hold. On the other hand, if  
                         ≿ 
                       is a simple preference (i.e., reflexive and complete), then its transitivity is equivalent to the fact that it satisfies  
                         
                            ( 
                            3 
                            , 
                            3 
                            ) 
                         
                      -Ferrers property.

Corollary 3.2 
                         
                            A reflexive relation is a total preorder if and only if it is weakly

                            -Ferrers.

                               A

                               ≿

                                  ( 
                                  3

Fig. 2 
                       describes the finite poset of the weak  
                         
                            ( 
                            m 
                            , 
                            n 
                            ) 
                         
                      -Ferrers properties, as derived from  Theorem 3.1 : the partial order is the implication between Ferrers properties, and is represented by an arrow. (In the  Appendix ,  Fig. 3 
                      , we provide a more accurate diagram of the implications among weak  
                         
                            ( 
                            m 
                            , 
                            n 
                            ) 
                         
                      -Ferrers properties, since we consider all possible combinations of these axioms.) The diagram displayed in  Fig. 2  shows that weak  
                         
                            ( 
                            m 
                            , 
                            n 
                            ) 
                         
                      -Ferrers properties yield a refinement of the hierarchy given in  Fig. 1  only for its upper segment (basically starting from the classical Ferrers property and the semitransitivity of a reflexive relation), since they are too strong to refine its lower segment. To obtain a refinement of the lower segment of the hierarchy given in  Fig. 1 , one has to employ combinations of the strict  
                         
                            ( 
                            m 
                            , 
                            n 
                            ) 
                         
                      -Ferrers properties (see   Fishburn, 1997; Öztürk, 2008 ).

Next, we reinforce the accuracy of the diagram in  Fig. 2  by proving that (i) all arrows cannot be reversed, and (ii) no other implication holds, apart from those obtained by taking the transitive closure of the diagram. To this aim, it suffices to show that the following facts hold:

In this paper we have presented a systematic treatment of the  
                         
                            ( 
                            m 
                            , 
                            n 
                            ) 
                         
                      -Ferrers properties, which generalize the property of Ferrers and the semitransitivity of a binary relation. The main motivation of our approach is that the two classical preference relations of interval orders and semiorders are defined using the  
                         
                            ( 
                            2 
                            , 
                            2 
                            ) 
                         
                      -Ferrers property and the  
                         
                            ( 
                            3 
                            , 
                            1 
                            ) 
                         
                      -Ferrers property. By extending the strict approach introduced in   Öztürk (2008) , here we distinguish two versions of  
                         
                            ( 
                            m 
                            , 
                            n 
                            ) 
                         
                      -Ferrers properties, namely:  weak , related to a reflexive binary relation, and  strict , related to its asymmetric part. It is well known that the two versions coincide for interval orders and semiorders: in fact, the strict and the weak  
                         
                            ( 
                            2 
                            , 
                            2 
                            ) 
                         
                      -Ferrers properties are equivalent to each other, and the same equivalence holds for the strict and the weak  
                         
                            ( 
                            3 
                            , 
                            1 
                            ) 
                         
                      -Ferrers properties.

Surprisingly, interval orders and semiorders are the only cases in which the two versions coincide: in fact, for  
                         m 
                         + 
                         n 
                         ≠ 
                         4 
                      , strict and weak  
                         
                            ( 
                            m 
                            , 
                            n 
                            ) 
                         
                      -Ferrers properties display an almost dual behavior (see  Lemmas 2.4–2.6 , as well as  Fig. 1 ). Specifically, as  
                         m 
                       and  
                         n 
                       increase, strict  
                         
                            ( 
                            m 
                            , 
                            n 
                            ) 
                         
                      -Ferrers properties tend to become weaker and weaker, whereas weak  
                         
                            ( 
                            m 
                            , 
                            n 
                            ) 
                         
                      -Ferrers properties become stronger and stronger. However, the strength of strict  
                         
                            ( 
                            m 
                            , 
                            n 
                            ) 
                         
                      -Ferrers properties is monotonically decreasing only under the assumption that the asymmetric preference is transitive, since otherwise their behavior is rather irregular. This irregularity is already apparent in the simplest case of the strict  
                         
                            ( 
                            m 
                            , 
                            1 
                            ) 
                         
                      -Ferrers properties (cf.   Examples 2.8–2.14 ). However, the behavior of strict  
                         
                            ( 
                            m 
                            , 
                            1 
                            ) 
                         
                      -Ferrers properties appears not to be erratic. In this respect, we conjecture that–rather unexpectedly–strict Ferrers properties “eventually stabilize”, in the following sense: the strict  
                         
                            ( 
                            m 
                            , 
                            1 
                            ) 
                         
                      -Ferrers property implies the strict  
                         
                            ( 
                            q 
                            , 
                            1 
                            ) 
                         
                      -property for all integers  
                         q 
                       larger than the “stabilizer” of  
                         m 
                      . The existence of the stabilizer of all integers would allow one to associate an almost monotonic notion of strength to all strict Ferrers properties, and arrange them into an infinite lattice (see  Conjecture 2.16 ).

The situation is remarkably different for weak  
                         
                            ( 
                            m 
                            , 
                            n 
                            ) 
                         
                      -Ferrers properties, since they invariantly display an increasing strength as  
                         m 
                       and  
                         n 
                       increase, culminating with the satisfaction of the transitive property: indeed, the weak  
                         
                            ( 
                            m 
                            , 
                            n 
                            ) 
                         
                      -Ferrers properties can be arranged into a finite poset under implication, having the weak  
                         
                            ( 
                            3 
                            , 
                            3 
                            ) 
                         
                      -Ferrers property as its maximum (see  Theorem 3.1  and  Examples 3.3–3.10 ). The partial order displayed in  Fig. 2  (for single  
                         
                            ( 
                            m 
                            , 
                            n 
                            ) 
                         
                      -Ferrers properties) and  Fig. 3  (for their combinations) is currently under investigation. In fact, it appears that this partial order can be further simplified in some special cases, namely, for extended preferences which admit a  linearly convex  representation with respect to an underlying total order (Giarlotta and Watson, preprint). Furthermore, it is still open the problem of obtaining forms of “interval representations” of extended preferences satisfying suitable weak  
                         
                            ( 
                            m 
                            , 
                            n 
                            ) 
                         
                      -Ferrers properties, e.g.,   strong interval orders  (which are weakly  
                         
                            ( 
                            3 
                            , 
                            2 
                            ) 
                         
                      -Ferrers) and  strong semiorders  (which are weakly  
                         
                            ( 
                            3 
                            , 
                            2 
                            ) 
                         
                      -Ferrers and  
                         
                            ( 
                            4 
                            , 
                            1 
                            ) 
                         
                      -Ferrers).

Finally, observe that strict and weak  
                         
                            ( 
                            m 
                            , 
                            n 
                            ) 
                         
                      -Ferrers properties can be combined to generate new types of preference relations. In particular, concerning the case of NaP-preference structures ( Giarlotta, in press; Giarlotta & Greco, 2013 ), one could require that the asymmetric part  
                         
                            
                               ≻ 
                            
                            
                               
                                  
                                  
                                     
                                        
                                        
                                           N 
                                        
                                     
                                  
                               
                            
                         
                       of the necessary component  
                         
                            
                               ≿ 
                            
                            
                               
                                  
                                  
                                     
                                        
                                        
                                           N 
                                        
                                     
                                  
                               
                            
                         
                       satisfies suitable strict  
                         
                            ( 
                            m 
                            , 
                            n 
                            ) 
                         
                      -Ferrers properties, whereas the possible component  
                         
                            
                               ≿ 
                            
                            
                               
                                  
                                  
                                     
                                        
                                        
                                           P 
                                        
                                     
                                  
                               
                            
                         
                       satisfies some weak  
                         
                            ( 
                            m 
                            , 
                            n 
                            ) 
                         
                      -Ferrers properties: this mixed approach becomes feasible in view of the recent introduction of the so-called  strict NaP-preferences  (Giarlotta, preprint).

The authors wish to thank the action-editor J.-P. Doignon and two anonymous referees for some useful comments and suggestions.

Fig. 3  presents the full structure of weak  
                         
                            ( 
                            m 
                            , 
                            n 
                            ) 
                         
                      -Ferrers properties for a reflexive binary relation on an arbitrary set  
                         A 
                      . All implications among combinations of weak Ferrers properties are denoted by an arrow.  Theorem 3.1  and  Examples 3.3–3.10  yield that no additional implications hold, apart from those obtained by taking the transitive closure of the diagram. According to this diagram, weak  
                         
                            ( 
                            m 
                            , 
                            n 
                            ) 
                         
                      -Ferrers properties appear to be useful in describing a “discrete evolution” of the transitivity of a weak preference: in fact, the binary relation at the bottom of the diagram (i.e., a simple preference) need not satisfy any type of pseudo-transitivity, whereas the one at the top of the diagram (i.e., a total preorder) fully satisfies the transitivity property.
